        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer17854255" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12175001956544784086">Looking over my shelves, I came across this book again and I can't believe I had rated it only three stars, barely categorized it, and went about my merry way.<br/><br/> No.<br/><br/> I remember reading this in one night, after crashing at a friend's house, after idly picking it up and suddenly dedicating the next however many hours of the night into the sickly morning to going all the way. All the way in it. Submerged. All the way to the bitter fucking end. <br/><br/> I think I wrote a long, detiled, passionate review of th</span>
  <span id="freeText12175001956544784086" style="display:none">Looking over my shelves, I came across this book again and I can't believe I had rated it only three stars, barely categorized it, and went about my merry way.<br /><br /> No.<br /><br /> I remember reading this in one night, after crashing at a friend's house, after idly picking it up and suddenly dedicating the next however many hours of the night into the sickly morning to going all the way. All the way in it. Submerged. All the way to the bitter fucking end. <br /><br /> I think I wrote a long, detiled, passionate review of this book a long time ago and the computer fucked up or something because I really can't see why I didn't write more about it once I'd added it to my shelves here.<br /><br /> It took me over. You know when you read a book and you start to feel caught up in its swell? Like the things the author or the character describes are somehow speaking <i> for </i> you in some ways that you can't quite understand, except that they are relating something personal unto them and yet they are also simultaneously describing the memories, dreams, reflections and sensations that you yourself have felt, running like telegrams under the thick, full folds of your unconscious? <br /><br /> Like the words are taken out of you, spoken for you, exhumed and exorcized in all their alien majesty back in black ink on flattened, whitened pieces of rippable pulp?<br /><br /> Forget categories, forget genres, forget classifications and taxonomies. This book is undoubtedly a fine example of 'queer discourse' (I'd seen it on more than a few curriculums to that effect and the person on whose shelf it rested was not really literary, probably a college text in a class she'd liked or something) and that is all perfectly well and good, but it transcends speaking from one narrowly defined social group and spoke robustly of its own humanity. <br /><br /> There's wisdom in this, I submit, and transcendence, and- perhaps- true enlightenment. Who's to say? Does it matter? Why should it? Why must it?<br /><br /> The fact that Winterson made her narrator genderless (a fact I remembered after all these years, excellently enough) speaks to that.<br /><br /> Look. Years before I read it something dreadful happened to somebody I love and though I loved them I couldn't love them, ultimately, enough. That has everything and nothing to do with what I want to write here but the plain fact is, whether Winterson knew it or not or even bothered to think about it or not or whether I'm putting words in her mouth by even saying this I knew EXACTLY what she meant by certain things she'd written herein. I'd lived it myself and this is precisely the way I'd have explained it, had I the courage and the brio Winterson needed and must have used to put this book together.<br /><br /> It's a little like Salinger's (or should I say Holden's) musing that a really good book is when you have the irrisitible urge to call the author up in the middle of the night and talk to them, be friendly with them; you feel like they are one of your own in some indescribable but irriducible way. I don't necessarily want or need to have Winterson over for tea or anything (though I'd like to) but it also feels like it wouldn't really matter. <br /><br /> Each sentence of this (novel) (memoir) (confession) rings with the truth that is ripped out of the core of things, the eddy of the actual, and each sentence is a hard diamond, glittering with the blood, the flesh, the yearning and the spirit. The deep heart's core. If those four words mean anything, they mean what they mean, and I'm glad that William Butler Yeats put them down and gave them some stately legitimacy. And for Winterson, too, of course, for having even more courage to write the book...<br /><br /> I didn't predict the hour, or the day, or the moment, or the person, or the author, but a very distinct and powerful moment in my personal history was connected by and to this book, before I'd even known of it at all it was there all the time, the perfect words to describe something millions know but I know in myself, by myself, and even then only from the outside, from the periphery, it didn't happen to me, you don't need to justify your suffering, it is there, it was there, there it will be...<br /><br /> Once there was a guy I worked with, didn't like him all that much, who sort of pronounced, unprompted, out of the blue, that I don't know 'real pain'. Well guess what, fucker, yeah I do. I *actually* do. <br /> <i> o yeah? </i> <br /> Yeah. <br /><i> what was it, then? </i> he snidely remarked.<br /> None of your goddamn business, I said. <br /> It wasn't. I don't have to justify that shit to you. Not to anyone, certainly, but especially not to your pompous, idiotic ass. You have my personal invitation to fuck right off into a barrel of snakes and broken glass. There was a pause. He knew. He backed down. We became friendlier later on, I decided I wasn't going to browbeat him and he didn't know what the hell he was talking about anyway. I prefer to make friends of enemies. He didn't know any better, and he was probably about twice my age. But I still hate him for that. And I should.<br /><br />...and the words were there, finally, unexpectedly, somewhere in the ether, written on the body.<br /><br /> I'm sorry, R. I love you, I loved you, but I am so deeply sorry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12175001956544784086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1929267447" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1929267447" />
    <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes"><img alt="aPriL does feral sometimes " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1478243665p2/3409532.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1929267447?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="aPriL does feral sometimes " class="user" itemprop="url" name="aPriL does feral sometimes " href="/user/show/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes">aPriL does feral sometimes </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3409532-april-does-feral-sometimes?shelf=literary">literary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1929267447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14602304244283315622">"Written on the Body" is an extremely thoughtful, poetic and emotionally authentic novel about the fictional narrator's love affairs with women. I am amazed I somewhat liked it. Not only is it a novel of intense and analyzed descriptions of emotional and physical love in romantic sexual relationships, woman after woman (I HATE the Romance genre generally, with few exceptions), but the book struck me at first that it might be written in a type of literary writing which I usually find unappealing.</span>
  <span id="freeText14602304244283315622" style="display:none">"Written on the Body" is an extremely thoughtful, poetic and emotionally authentic novel about the fictional narrator's love affairs with women. I am amazed I somewhat liked it. Not only is it a novel of intense and analyzed descriptions of emotional and physical love in romantic sexual relationships, woman after woman (I HATE the Romance genre generally, with few exceptions), but the book struck me at first that it might be written in a type of literary writing which I usually find unappealing. The story actually ended up much more full of genuine life and realistic analysis, more like a journal of thoughtful musings and honest regrets over failed relationships, especially for the one that got away which really hurt. <br /><br />Many reviewers have commented on how the author, Jeanette Winterson, chose to keep the narrator's sexual gender a secret, but the novel has won both the Ferro-Grumley Award for Lesbian Fiction (1994) and the Lambda Literary Award for Lesbian Fiction (1994), probably because women's bodies are described in some detail and with a lot of passion by the nameless narrator. <br /><br />Usually authors writing in the approved Ivory-Tower, academic-scholar style feel to me like they are pandering to other academic scholars of literature instead of writing an authentic and illuminating book. However, I think 'Written on the Body' never drops into that overly intellectualized and contrived poetic cerebral agony manner of writing, which so often poisons intelligentsia-favored books for me (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/22929741.The_Argonauts" title="The Argonauts by Maggie Nelson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Argonauts</a> being such a one in the non-fiction category, and the fictional <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9717.The_Unbearable_Lightness_of_Being" title="The Unbearable Lightness of Being by Milan Kundera" rel="nofollow noopener">The Unbearable Lightness of Being</a> as another). Instead, Winterson's novel is beautifully written, with only rueful honesty or joy expressed by her nameless character about a number of love affairs. My problem with the book is entirely due to that I am not much of a fan of Romance literature, whether it be lowbrow or of the highest quality. I would have enjoyed the book more as a short story, I think.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14602304244283315622" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2082412?book_show_action=true">Jun 18, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">romantics.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=not-my-thing">not-my-thing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=hey-shorty">hey-shorty</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=not-by-a-white-guy">not-by-a-white-guy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32865-taylor?shelf=lgbtqa">lgbtqa</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2082412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5096173045674850944">Simply put, this is the story of someone (man or woman, who knows? My guess is man, but it doesn't really matter) who is in love with a woman named Louise. They have to overcome a series of hurdles, such as their relationships with other people and a terminal disease.<br/><br/>It's a very quick read - I blazed through this in about 2-3 days of reading on the subway. A quick reader could probably finish in one day of dedicated reading. However, despite how easy it is to read, it's also a little overwhelmin</span>
  <span id="freeText5096173045674850944" style="display:none">Simply put, this is the story of someone (man or woman, who knows? My guess is man, but it doesn't really matter) who is in love with a woman named Louise. They have to overcome a series of hurdles, such as their relationships with other people and a terminal disease.<br /><br />It's a very quick read - I blazed through this in about 2-3 days of reading on the subway. A quick reader could probably finish in one day of dedicated reading. However, despite how easy it is to read, it's also a little overwhelming. The language is so pretty, so flowery, so ornate, that I found myself tiring of it rather quickly. I love beautiful language as much as the next person, but this was just too much, too over the top. I wanted medium, she gave me well done. Part of the reason I read it so fast is because I just wanted it to be over with. Not because I didn't like it, just because it was way too flowery, too sweet.<br /><br />I could've done with the first 110 or whatever pages as the entire novel and been completely satisfied. I ran out of interest about 3/4 the way through when she started describing each part of the woman's body with 2 page passages. I know that's supposed to be the heart of the novel, but after a couple sections, it was just so damn boring - I started skimming them. It got more interesting when the story picked up again, but I saw little point to that addition to the plot.<br /><br />If you like flowery, delicate writing, you'll eat this up, but in terms of an overall solid body of work, <i>The Passion</i> is better.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5096173045674850944" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_47477615" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/47477615" />
    <a title="Kate" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1914270-kate"><img alt="Kate" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1385149026p2/1914270.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/47477615?book_show_action=true">Feb 25, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/1914270-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1914270-kate?shelf=re-read-it">re-read-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1914270-kate?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1914270-kate?shelf=signed-by-author">signed-by-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer47477615" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7002686576872167533">This book was my first introduction to Jeanette Winterson's work. I read it in college for a class on contemporary fiction. I have to admit, I was blown away by her writing style, it is so unique. This book moved me to no end. I didn't care if the narrator was male or female, it really didn't matter. The way Jeanette was able to craft the words on paper to make me feel what the narrator was feeling and experiencing was what made this book memorable to me. Now I'm totally hooked.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_37043224" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/37043224" />
    <a title="Janelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/975335-janelle"><img alt="Janelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296657206p2/975335.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/37043224?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janelle" href="/user/show/975335-janelle">Janelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer37043224" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16064544633254191362">I feel like Jeanette Winterson is someone I'm supposed to like, but in reality, I find her work to be frustratingly uneven and myopic. Maybe I'm not highbrow enough for her stuff, or maybe it's just too naval-gazing! This prompted some interesting discussion in my book group, though. Some people adored the nonspecificity of the narrator's gender. I thought it came off as a bit of a literary trick, though. Still, it was interesting that all the lesbian readers thought the narrator was female, and</span>
  <span id="freeText16064544633254191362" style="display:none">I feel like Jeanette Winterson is someone I'm supposed to like, but in reality, I find her work to be frustratingly uneven and myopic. Maybe I'm not highbrow enough for her stuff, or maybe it's just too naval-gazing! This prompted some interesting discussion in my book group, though. Some people adored the nonspecificity of the narrator's gender. I thought it came off as a bit of a literary trick, though. Still, it was interesting that all the lesbian readers thought the narrator was female, and all the straight women readers thought the narrator was male. Pretty typical, I guess.<br /><br />I find Winterson's actual life more fascinating then her works, I'm afraid.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16064544633254191362" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2343478247" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2343478247" />
    <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all"><img alt="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406361683p2/20738920.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2343478247?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)" href="/user/show/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all">Orinoco Womble (tidy bag and all)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=heavy-as-lead">heavy-as-lead</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=d-is-for-dysfunctional">d-is-for-dysfunctional</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=life-s-too-short">life-s-too-short</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=run-save-yourselves">run-save-yourselves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/20738920-orinoco-womble-tidy-bag-and-all?shelf=nmsot">nmsot</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2343478247" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer188459385077862046">Ugh.<br/>Did not like it, at all. Self-indulgent stream of consciousness that reads like a LiveJournal blog has never been my thing. None of the characters was remotely sympathetic, each being as selfish as all the others, eager to blame everyone, anyone else for their own self-inflicted miseries. The author couldn't even be bothered to punctuate half the sentences, and apparently the editor didn't care, either. <br/><br/>I read it because I had to. If you have any sense, you won't. Even if you're supposed to.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('2Bgtcn6ltnYVHFf67ZqSpgqNtoRx/6tYHjUd4YvdeFbiPkSgxft/HrI345sab5h53NJRTL9yJT9FS1tQRp6KzA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('z9TqS+MwukWZ43Qo9XZw25tU52dibNxKCk+FyP+d7Qr18oOZWG5zLT7IwEkCg3oETQsAr6zhUi1RMcN5Mt4fkA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Here was a woman who sought the freedom to act out her sexual and emotional desires with the same guiltless, &quot;amoral&quot; abandon that men have always claimed for themselves. When Nin began publishing sections of her diary in 1966, this aspect of her life was excised, though clearly there was more than could be told at the time concerning her relationships with Henry Miller and his wife, June, with the writer and actor Antonin Artaud, with her analysts Rene Allendy and Otto Rank, and - most important - with her father. Here now is the previously missing portion of Nin\'s life in the crucial years from 1932 to 1934, the shattering psychological drama that drove her to seek absolution from her psychoanalysts for the ultimate transgression. In its raw exposure of a woman\'s struggle to come to terms with herself, to find salvation in the very act of writing, Incest unveils an Anais Nin without masks and secrets, yet in the end still After the murder of her daughter Iphegenia by her husband Agamemnon, Klytaimestra exacts a mother’s revenge, murdering Agamemnon and his mistress, Kassandra. Displeased with Klytaimestra’s actions, Apollo calls on her son, Orestes, to avenge his father’s death with the help of his sister Elektra. In the end, Orestes, driven mad by the Furies for his bloody betrayal of family, and Elektra are condemned to death by the people of Argos, and must justify their actions—signaling a call to change in society, a shift from the capricious governing of the gods to the rule of manmade law.\n\nCarson’s accomplished rendering combines elements of contemporary vernacular with the traditional structures and rhetoric of Greek tragedy, opening up the plays to a modern audience. In addition to its accessibility, the wit and dazzling morbidity of her prose sheds new light on the saga for scholars.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/9399.Jeanette_Winterson">About Jeanette Winterson</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7570381736476670748">Novelist Jeanette Winterson was born in Manchester, England in 1959. She was adopted and brought up in Accrington, Lancashire, in the north of England. Her strict Pentecostal Evangelist upbringing provides the background to her acclaimed first novel, <i>Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit</i>, published in 1985. She graduated from St Catherine's College, Oxford, and moved to London where she worked as an assi</span>
  <span id="freeText7570381736476670748" style="display:none">Novelist Jeanette Winterson was born in Manchester, England in 1959. She was adopted and brought up in Accrington, Lancashire, in the north of England. Her strict Pentecostal Evangelist upbringing provides the background to her acclaimed first novel, <i>Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit</i>, published in 1985. She graduated from St Catherine's College, Oxford, and moved to London where she worked as an assistant editor at Pandora Press.<br /><br />One of the most original voices in British fiction to emerge during the 1980s, Winterson was named as one of the 20 "Best of Young British Writers" in a promotion run jointly between the literary magazine Granta and the Book Marketing Council. <br /><br />She adapted <i>Oranges Are Not the Only Fruit</i> for BBC television in 1990 and also wrote "Great Moments in Aviation," a television screenplay directed by Beeban Kidron for BBC2 in 1994. She is editor of a series of new editions of novels by Virginia Woolf published in the UK by Vintage. She is a regular contributor of reviews and articles to many newspapers and journals and has a regular column published in The Guardian. Her radio drama includes the play Text Message, broadcast by BBC Radio in November 2001. <br /><br />Winterson lives in Gloucestershire and London. Her work is published in 28 countries. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7570381736476670748" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

Why would I want them to?&rdquo;</span>
